<span id="freeTextContainer1936757943318794845">My least favourite Murakami so far. <br/><br/>I liked Aomame's story more than Tengo’s, but neither had me believe the characters or want to know more about them - which is a problem when their relationship is supposed to drive the book. As always with Murakami, there were a lot of tangents, mysterious side-characters, and loose threads, but this time they felt particularly annoying and unnecessary. The sex-elements also definitely went too far this time. I liked the little people and the air chrysalis, y</span>
  <span id="freeText1936757943318794845" style="display:none">My least favourite Murakami so far. <br /><br />I liked Aomame's story more than Tengo’s, but neither had me believe the characters or want to know more about them - which is a problem when their relationship is supposed to drive the book. As always with Murakami, there were a lot of tangents, mysterious side-characters, and loose threads, but this time they felt particularly annoying and unnecessary. The sex-elements also definitely went too far this time. I liked the little people and the air chrysalis, yet the political/religious embedding blew the story up beyond what it could handle. <br /><br />I do have to say that I read this book over the course of a few months (it's too heavy to carry it around, and also too dragging), which might have added to it - my bad! <br /><br />I've read a lot of good reviews about 1Q84 and believe many Murakami-heads consider this one of the best - I am curious to know why (for context, my favourite is Hard Boiled Wonderland, and I've read about half of his books so far). If this was one of your favs, please let me know! :-)</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3086023556" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12129037446472645780">The story of 1Q84 is divided between two protagonists, mathematical genius and would-be writer Tengo and the enigmatic Aomame, and through their alternate chapters these two stories overlap and converge. Tengo is roped in to help rewrite a story by a young girl, and before long mysterious events begin to happen that sweep him along with them.<br/><br/>This is a bizarre and often brilliant book, packed with strange symbolism and a complex plot which reveals itself bit by bit. It is original and imaginative</span>
  <span id="freeText12129037446472645780" style="display:none">The story of 1Q84 is divided between two protagonists, mathematical genius and would-be writer Tengo and the enigmatic Aomame, and through their alternate chapters these two stories overlap and converge. Tengo is roped in to help rewrite a story by a young girl, and before long mysterious events begin to happen that sweep him along with them.<br /><br />This is a bizarre and often brilliant book, packed with strange symbolism and a complex plot which reveals itself bit by bit. It is original and imaginative, mixing fantasy and reality. I am looking forward to concluding the story with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12337749.1Q84_Book_3" title="1Q84 Book 3 by Haruki Murakami" rel="nofollow noopener">1Q84: Book 3</a> </span>

    <div class="elementListBrownSeparator"></div>



  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_226660760" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/226660760" />
    <a title="Sam Quixote" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote"><img alt="Sam Quixote" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529183438p2/5759543.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/226660760?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Quixote" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Quixote" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote">Sam Quixote</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781846554070" href="/book/show/9911160-1q84">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer226660760" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16158389139163524247">SPOILERS<br/><br/>The year is 1984 and a woman called Aomame on her way to an important meeting gets stuck in a traffic jam and, abandoning her taxi, heads to a tunnel with an emergency exit. Unknowingly Aomame has left the world of 1984 and entered into a subtly different world called 1Q84. <br/><br/>A man called Tengo is an aspiring but unpublished writer who is approached by his eccentric publisher to ghost-write a short novel by a 17 year old girl entitled "Air Chrysalis". Once the book is published, strange th</span>
  <span id="freeText16158389139163524247" style="display:none">SPOILERS<br /><br />The year is 1984 and a woman called Aomame on her way to an important meeting gets stuck in a traffic jam and, abandoning her taxi, heads to a tunnel with an emergency exit. Unknowingly Aomame has left the world of 1984 and entered into a subtly different world called 1Q84. <br /><br />A man called Tengo is an aspiring but unpublished writer who is approached by his eccentric publisher to ghost-write a short novel by a 17 year old girl entitled "Air Chrysalis". Once the book is published, strange things start happening and both Aomame and Tengo look up into the night sky and see two moons. <br /><br />Throw in some assassinations, child molestation, secret cults, otherworldly powers, a town of cats and strange beings called "the Little People" and you've got an idea of the hugely ambitious, complex and packed book that is Books 1 and 2 of Haruki Murakami's "1Q84".<br /><br />There's a lot going on in the book that I felt were utterly original and fascinating to read about. First off, Aomame is an emotionless assassin who targets only men who have abused their wives/women in general and kills them with a specially designed ice-pick to the back of the neck, making their deaths look like natural causes. I thought Murakami handled this story excellently, proving he could write thrilling sequences with suspense and make it look easy. <br /><br />There's a religious cult called "Sakigake" who have something to do with "the Little People" and Aomame targets the head of the cult, simply called "Leader", which forms a large part of the second book and reading it was pure joy. Murakami depicts the character of Leader as semi-monstrous, inhuman in many aspects, and leading up to the reveal of the character was some of the best scene-setting any writer could do. His eventual encounter with Aomame too is very exciting and well written. <br /><br />I think the effectiveness of this novel lies in the way Murakami writes the book as a straight story, with two seemingly normal people, and then mentions odd things like "Little People" and "Leader" with the barest hint of what he's talking about, leaving it to the reader to imagine what they look like/represent, or actually are. <br /><br />Tengo's story was less dramatic but no less compelling. Murakami is a writer who can make the assassination of a cult leader and a man preparing dinner both seem hypnotising to the reader. The glimpse Murakami offers into the world of Japanese publishing is interesting and the 17 year old writer whom Tengo is ghosting for, Fuka-Eri, is an interesting character who links Tengo's story to Aomame's and the overall story of this strange nether-world. <br /><br />The only complaint I would have, and it's a fairly large one, lies with the main motivations of the two leads. They are both aware of each other as they were both at school together in the same class when they were 10. Shortly after the briefest of meetings they go their separate ways and wind up leading very different lives but they continue to pine for each other 20 years later! It just didn't seem real to me that two people who didn't even have much of an acquaintance would base so much of their actions, as well as their hopes and dreams, upon someone they barely knew when they were pre-adolescent children! It just doesn't seem even half plausible. And in the 623 pages of this book, they don't meet once, yet Aomame is willing to die for Tengo! Why?!<br /><br />That said, the story was engrossing and the writing of Murakami's highest quality. The imagery alone is worth reading this book for and the simple scenes within scenes, what some people will no doubt class as overwritten. I've been a fan of Murakami's for many years now and I love his style of writing. He's going to tell you a story and he's going to tell it at his own pace. Whether you keep up or not is up to you. I love his easy going pace and the balance of complexity and simplicity that he brings to each book. <br /><br />I think any Murakami fan would pick this book up unquestioningly and they would be right to do so - this is easily his most ambitious work and needs to be read to be believed. For someone approaching Murakami for the first time, I would suggest earlier works like "The Wind-Up Bird Chronicle" or "A Wild Sheep Chase" to give you a flavour of what this writer writes. Asking someone to tackle a 1000 page story when they're not acquainted with the writer is asking a lot. <br /><br />Despite its faults, and there are definitely a few, I think looked at overall it's a fantastic achievement and a great read. Forget the connotations of Orwell or anyone saying it's Murakami's version of "1984" because it's not, nor is it a love story - it's Murakami's version of a Haruki Murakami story and in the end that's more than enough. <br /><br />Onto Book 3! </span>
  <a data-text-id="16158389139163524247" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('JDFJaoHvHQX+3Ycn+jt9Q/YV9IxtTiJIm/bFowlQ1AC20HT9G3tKf4xekKla3BPs7BGiKXbAlNL/v9VP56yh3Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jqpPqcwacMfkSBYTcXVy2Ll5eYyBA0foPI3iOvFDBaccS3I+Vo4nvZbLAZ3Rkhx3o30vKZqN8XJYxPLWH79weg==')})
  }
//]]>

In Le dita verdi, dall\'atmosfera fiabesca, una nonna chiaroveggente insegna alla nipote il linguaggio delle piante, che è quello della cura e dell\'amore. Per uno dei personaggi, dietro un momento di gioia e spensieratezza è in agguato un\'esperienza di lutto. Toni tra il gotico e il dark in La mummia, un racconto attraversato dall\'ombra di un serial killer, che si trasforma in un\'inattesa e toccante love story. Stereotipo della divorziata indipendente dell’alta società nipponica del dopoguerra, dove il desiderio di occidentalizzazione si contrappone ad antiche tradizioni e pregiudizi, Taeko non vuole rinunciare al proprio stile di vita né alla libertà. Poi, una sera, incontra il giovane Senkichi in un gay bar e l’attrazione è fatale. Una magia che scaturisce dalla carne fresca e virile del ragazzo, dai suoi muscoli tesi, dai lineamenti fieri del viso. La vita di Taeko cambia in un batter d’occhio: proprio lei che aveva sempre voluto solo avventure si ritrova irrimediabilmente in balìa di un giovane tanto bello quanto misterioso. Ne scaturisce un gioco perfido e ossessivo. Ma chi è davvero la vittima? Chi il carnefice? Mishima mette in scena il mercato dei sentimenti. Può la passione essere una merce tanto preziosa da annebbiare anche la più lucida delle menti? E quando ci innamoriamo, come facciamo a capire di non essere soltanto caduti nell’ingannevole trappola dei sensi? Non resta che frequentare quella “scuola della carne” che è la vita. Jeden Tag fällt die Welt um sie herum ein Stück mehr auseinander: Städte ohne Strom und Wasser werden zu Todesfallen, Krankheit, Hunger und Gewalt breiten sich aus. Die zivilisierte Welt kollabiert.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/3354.Haruki_Murakami">About Haruki Murakami</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/3354.Haruki_Murakami"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1539035376p3/3354.jpg);'></div>

<a href="/author/show/3354.Haruki_Murakami">Haruki Murakami
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer16331602505219724664"><b>Murakami</b> Haruki (Japanese: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11882216' rel='nofollow noopener'>村上 春樹</a>) is a popular contemporary Japanese writer and translator. His work has been described as <i>'easily accessible, yet profoundly complex'</i>. He can be located on Facebook at: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.facebook.com/harukimurakamiauthor'>https://www.facebook.com/harukimuraka...</a><br/><br/>Since childhood, Murakami has been heavily influenced by Western culture, particularly Western music and literature. He grew up reading a range of works by Am</span>
  <span id="freeText16331602505219724664" style="display:none"><b>Murakami</b> Haruki (Japanese: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11882216" rel="nofollow noopener">村上 春樹</a>) is a popular contemporary Japanese writer and translator. His work has been described as <i>'easily accessible, yet profoundly complex'</i>. He can be located on Facebook at: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/harukimurakamiauthor">https://www.facebook.com/harukimuraka...</a><br /><br />Since childhood, Murakami has been heavily influenced by Western culture, particularly Western music and literature. He grew up reading a range of works by American writers, such as Kurt Vonnegut and Richard Brautigan, and he is often distinguished from other Japanese writers by his Western influences.<br /><br />Murakami studied drama at Waseda University in Tokyo, where he met his wife, Yoko. His first job was at a record store, which is where one of his main characters, Toru Watanabe in <i>Norwegian Wood</i>, works. Shortly before finishing his studies, Murakami opened the coffeehouse <i>'Peter Cat'</i> which was a jazz bar in the evening in Kokubunji, Tokyo with his wife.<br /><br />Many of his novels have themes and titles that invoke classical music, such as the three books making up <i>The Wind-Up Bird Chronicle</i>: <i>The Thieving Magpie</i> (after Rossini's opera), <i>Bird as Prophet</i> (after a piano piece by Robert Schumann usually known in English as <i>The Prophet Bird</i>), and <i>The Bird-Catcher</i> (a character in Mozart's opera <i>The Magic Flute</i>). Some of his novels take their titles from songs: <i>Dance, Dance, Dance</i> (after The Dells' song, although it is widely thought it was titled after the <i>Beach Boys</i> tune), <i>Norwegian Wood</i> (after The Beatles' song) and <i>South of the Border, West of the Sun</i> (the first part being the title of a song by Nat King Cole).<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16331602505219724664" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
